					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Food produced from cacao seeds</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Chocolate (disambiguation)">Chocolate (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">



</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Three_Bars_(1).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Three_Bars_%281%29.jpg/250px-Three_Bars_%281%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Three_Bars_%281%29.jpg/500px-Three_Bars_%281%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="7216" data-file-height="5412" /></a><figcaption>Chocolate bars in dark, white, and milk variants (top to bottom).</figcaption></figure>
<p><b>Chocolate</b> is a food made from roasted and ground <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cocoa beans</a> that can be a liquid, solid, or paste, either by itself or to <a href="/wiki/Flavoring" title="Flavoring">flavor</a> other foods. Cocoa beans are the processed seeds of the cacao tree (<i><a href="/wiki/Theobroma_cacao" title="Theobroma cacao">Theobroma cacao</a></i>). They are usually <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean_fermentation" title="Cocoa bean fermentation">fermented</a> to develop the flavor, then dried, cleaned, and roasted. The shell is removed to reveal nibs, which are ground to <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_liquor" title="Chocolate liquor">chocolate liquor</a>: unadulterated chocolate in rough form. The liquor can be processed to separate its two components, <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_solids" title="Cocoa solids">cocoa solids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_butter" title="Cocoa butter">cocoa butter</a>, or shaped and sold as unsweetened <a href="/wiki/Baking_chocolate" title="Baking chocolate">baking chocolate</a>. By adding sugar, sweetened chocolates are produced, which can be sold simply as <a href="/wiki/Dark_chocolate" title="Dark chocolate">dark chocolate</a>, or, with the addition of <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, can be made into <a href="/wiki/Milk_chocolate" title="Milk chocolate">milk chocolate</a>. Making milk chocolate with cocoa butter and without cocoa solids produces <a href="/wiki/White_chocolate" title="White chocolate">white chocolate</a>. 
</p><p>Chocolate is one of the most popular food types and flavors in the world, and many foodstuffs involving chocolate exist, particularly <a href="/wiki/Dessert" title="Dessert">desserts</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream" title="Ice cream">ice creams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_cake" title="Chocolate cake">cakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mousse" title="Mousse">mousse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_chip_cookie" title="Chocolate chip cookie">cookies</a>. Many <a href="/wiki/Candy" title="Candy">candies</a> are filled with or coated with sweetened chocolate. <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_bar" title="Chocolate bar">Chocolate bars</a>, either made of solid chocolate or other ingredients coated in chocolate, are eaten as snacks. Gifts of chocolate molded into different shapes (such as eggs, hearts, and coins) are traditional on certain holidays, including Christmas, <a href="/wiki/Easter_egg#Chocolate" title="Easter egg">Easter</a>, Valentine's Day, <a href="/wiki/Hanukkah_gelt" title="Hanukkah gelt">Hanukkah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Fitr" title="Eid al-Fitr">Eid al-Fitr</a>. Chocolate is also used in cold and hot beverages, such as <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_milk" title="Chocolate milk">chocolate milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hot_chocolate" title="Hot chocolate">hot chocolate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_liqueur" title="Chocolate liqueur">chocolate liqueur</a>.
</p><p>The cacao tree was first used as a source for food in what is today Ecuador at least 5,300 years ago. <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerican</a> civilizations widely consumed cacao beverages, and in the 16th century, one of these beverages, chocolate, was introduced to Europe. Until the 19th century, chocolate was a drink consumed by societal elite. After then, technological and cocoa production changes led to chocolate becoming a solid, mass-consumed food. In the 21st century, cocoa beans for most chocolate are produced in West African countries, particularly <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_production_in_Ivory_Coast" title="Cocoa production in Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_production_in_Ghana" title="Cocoa production in Ghana">Ghana</a>, which contribute about 60% of the world's cocoa supply. The presence of child labor, particularly child slavery and trafficking, in cocoa bean production in these countries has received significant media attention.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Kakaw_(Mayan_word).png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Kakaw_%28Mayan_word%29.png/250px-Kakaw_%28Mayan_word%29.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Kakaw_%28Mayan_word%29.png/400px-Kakaw_%28Mayan_word%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cb/Kakaw_%28Mayan_word%29.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="300" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Maya_script" title="Maya script">Maya glyph</a> for cacao</figcaption></figure>
<p><i>Chocolate</i> is a Spanish <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanword</a>, first recorded in English in 1604,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>a<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in Spanish in 1579.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESwantonde&#95;Ávilavan&#95;Doesburg2010435&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwantonde_Ávilavan_Doesburg2010435-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The word's origins beyond this are contentious.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESwanton202423&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwanton202423-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite a popular belief that <i>chocolate</i> derives from the <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a> word <span title="Classical Nahuatl-language text"><i lang="nci">chocolatl</i></span>, early texts documenting the Nahuatl word for chocolate drink use a different term, <span title="Classical Nahuatl-language text"><i lang="nci">cacahuatl</i></span><i>,</i> meaning "cacao water". Several alternatives have therefore been proposed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Crossing&#95;the&#95;Language&#95;Barrier&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Crossing_the_Language_Barrier-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In one, chocolate is derived from the hypothetical Nahuatl word <span title="Classical Nahuatl-language text"><i lang="nci">xocoatl</i></span>, meaning "bitter drink". Scholars <a href="/wiki/Michael_D._Coe" title="Michael D. Coe">Michael</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sophie_Coe" title="Sophie Coe">Sophie Coe</a> consider this unlikely, saying that there is no clear reason why the 'sh' sound represented by 'x' would change to 'ch', or why an 'l' would be added.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Crossing&#95;the&#95;Language&#95;Barrier&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Crossing_the_Language_Barrier-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another theory suggests that <i>chocolate</i> comes from <i>chocolatl</i>, meaning 'hot water' in a <a href="/wiki/Mayan_languages" title="Mayan languages">Mayan language</a>. However, there is no evidence of the form 'chocol' being used to mean hot.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Crossing&#95;the&#95;Language&#95;Barrier&#95;5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Crossing_the_Language_Barrier-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the uncertainty about its Nahuatl origin, there is some agreement that chocolate likely derives from the <a href="/wiki/Nawat_language" title="Nawat language">Nawat</a> word <i>chikola:tl</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKaufmanJusteson2007193&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufmanJusteson2007193-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Whether <i>chikola:tl</i> means 'cacao-beater', referring to whisking cocoa to create foam, is contested, as the meaning of <i>chico</i> is unknown.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKaufmanJusteson2007218DakinWichmann200063&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaufmanJusteson2007218DakinWichmann200063-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to anthropologist Kathryn Sampeck, chocolate originally referred to one cacao beverage among many, which included <a href="/wiki/Annatto" title="Annatto">annatto</a> and was made in what is today Guatemala. According to Sampeck, it became the generic word for cacao beverages <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1580</span>, when the Izalcos from that area were the most notable producers of cacao.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESampeckThayn201778–79&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampeckThayn201778–79-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_chocolate" title="History of chocolate">History of chocolate</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Aztec._Man_Carrying_a_Cacao_Pod,_1440-1521.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Aztec._Man_Carrying_a_Cacao_Pod%2C_1440-1521.jpg/250px-Aztec._Man_Carrying_a_Cacao_Pod%2C_1440-1521.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="254" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Aztec._Man_Carrying_a_Cacao_Pod%2C_1440-1521.jpg/330px-Aztec._Man_Carrying_a_Cacao_Pod%2C_1440-1521.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Aztec._Man_Carrying_a_Cacao_Pod%2C_1440-1521.jpg/500px-Aztec._Man_Carrying_a_Cacao_Pod%2C_1440-1521.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1150" data-file-height="1536" /></a><figcaption>Mexica. <i>Man Carrying a Cacao Pod</i>, 1440–1521</figcaption></figure>
<p>Evidence for the domestication of the <a href="/wiki/Theobroma_cacao" title="Theobroma cacao">cacao tree</a> exists as early as 5300 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, in present-day southeast <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Mayo-Chinchipe" title="Mayo-Chinchipe">Mayo-Chinchipe</a> culture, before it was introduced to <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is unknown when chocolate was first consumed as opposed to other cacao-based drinks, and there is evidence the <a href="/wiki/Olmecs" title="Olmecs">Olmecs</a>, the earliest known major Mesoamerican civilization, fermented the sweet pulp surrounding the cacao beans into an <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcoholic beverage</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013The&#95;Tree&#95;of&#95;the&#95;Food&#95;of&#95;the&#95;Gods&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013The_Tree_of_the_Food_of_the_Gods-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECollins2022301–302&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2022301–302-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Chocolate was extremely important to several Mesoamerican societies,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMacLeod2000636&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacLeod2000636-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and cacao was considered a gift from the gods by the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Mayans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aztec_Empire" title="Aztec Empire">Aztecs</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEVail200810&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVail200810-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECollins2022160–162&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2022160–162-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cocoa bean</a> was used as a currency across civilizations and was used in ceremonies, as a tribute to leaders and gods and as a medicine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013The&#95;Maya&#95;on&#95;the&#95;Eve&#95;of&#95;the&#95;Conquest&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013The_Maya_on_the_Eve_of_the_Conquest-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELeissle201834&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeissle201834-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe201395&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe201395-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPresilla200912,&#95;16,&#95;22&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPresilla200912,_16,_22-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEAguilar-Moreno2006274&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAguilar-Moreno2006274-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGrivetti2008b68&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrivetti2008b68-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chocolate in Mesoamerica was a bitter drink, flavored with additives such as <a href="/wiki/Vanilla" title="Vanilla">vanilla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cymbopetalum_penduliflorum" title="Cymbopetalum penduliflorum">earflower</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chili_pepper" title="Chili pepper">chili</a>, and was capped with a dark brown foam created by pouring the liquid from a height between containers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Flavorings,&#95;Spices,&#95;and&#95;Other&#95;Additions&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Flavorings,_Spices,_and_Other_Additions-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Cacao&#95;Preparation&#95;among&#95;the&#95;Late&#95;Maya&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Cacao_Preparation_among_the_Late_Maya-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Lords&#95;of&#95;the&#95;Forest:&#95;The&#95;Classic&#95;Maya&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Lords_of_the_Forest:_The_Classic_Maya-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Spanish <a href="/wiki/Conquistador" title="Conquistador">conquistador</a> <a href="/wiki/Hern%C3%A1n_Cort%C3%A9s" title="Hernán Cortés">Hernán Cortés</a> may have been the first European to encounter chocolate when he observed it in the court of <a href="/wiki/Moctezuma_II" title="Moctezuma II">Moctezuma II</a> in 1520.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGrivetti2008a100&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrivetti2008a100-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It proved to be an acquired taste,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECollins2022285–288&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2022285–288-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTENorton200415&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorton200415-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and it took until 1585 for the first official recording of a shipment of cocoa beans to Europe.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Cacao&#95;in&#95;Spain:&#95;&quot;Chocolate&#95;Brought&#95;to&#95;Perfection&quot;&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Cacao_in_Spain:_&quot;Chocolate_Brought_to_Perfection&quot;-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chocolate was believed to be an <a href="/wiki/Aphrodisiac" title="Aphrodisiac">aphrodisiac</a> and medicine, and spread across Europe in the 17th century, sweetened, served warm and flavored with familiar spices.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Chocolate&#95;and&#95;the&#95;English&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Chocolate_and_the_English-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013New&#95;Spain&#95;and&#95;Central&#95;America&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013New_Spain_and_Central_America-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Crossing&#95;the&#95;Taste&#95;Barrier&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Crossing_the_Taste_Barrier-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Religious orders played a significant role in its dissemination.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>
 It was initially primarily consumed by the elite, with expensive cocoa supplied by colonial plantations in the Americas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Chocolate&#95;and&#95;the&#95;English&#95;29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Chocolate_and_the_English-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the 18th century, it was considered southern European, <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocratic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a>, and was still produced in a similar way to the way it had been produced by the Aztecs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Chapter&#95;7&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Chapter_7-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p><figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Fry%27s_Chocolate_Cream_split.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Fry%27s_Chocolate_Cream_split.jpg/250px-Fry%27s_Chocolate_Cream_split.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Fry%27s_Chocolate_Cream_split.jpg/500px-Fry%27s_Chocolate_Cream_split.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="998" /></a><figcaption>One of the first mass-produced chocolate bars, <a href="/wiki/Fry%27s_Chocolate_Cream" title="Fry&#39;s Chocolate Cream">Fry's Chocolate Cream</a>, was produced by <a href="/wiki/J._S._Fry_%26_Sons" title="J. S. Fry &amp; Sons">Fry's</a> in 1866.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chocolates&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chocolates-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>Starting in the 18th century, chocolate production was improved. In the 19th century, engine-powered <a href="/wiki/Milling_(machining)" title="Milling (machining)">milling</a> was developed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESnyderOlsenBrindle2008612&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnyderOlsenBrindle2008612-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Quaker&#95;Capitalists&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Quaker_Capitalists-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1828, <a href="/wiki/Coenraad_Johannes_van_Houten" title="Coenraad Johannes van Houten">Coenraad Johannes van Houten</a> received a patent for a process making <a href="/wiki/Dutch_process_cocoa" title="Dutch process cocoa">Dutch cocoa</a>. This removed cocoa butter from <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_liquor" title="Chocolate liquor">chocolate liquor</a> (the product of milling), and permitted large scale production of chocolate.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013A&#95;Break&#95;with&#95;the&#95;Past:&#95;Van&#95;Houten&#39;s&#95;Inventions&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013A_Break_with_the_Past:_Van_Houten&#39;s_Inventions-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other developments in the 19th century, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Melanger" title="Melanger">melanger</a></i> (a mixing machine), modern <a href="/wiki/Milk_chocolate" title="Milk chocolate">milk chocolate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Conching" title="Conching">conching</a> process to make chocolate smoother and change the flavor meant a worker in 1890 could produce fifty times more chocolate with the same labor than they could before the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, and chocolate became a food to be eaten rather than drunk.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Switzerland:&#95;Land&#95;of&#95;Cows&#95;and&#95;Chocolate&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Switzerland:_Land_of_Cows_and_Chocolate-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As production moved from the Americas to Asia and Africa, mass markets in Western nations for chocolate opened up.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEClarence-Smith200048&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarence-Smith200048-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 20th century, British chocolate producers including <a href="/wiki/Cadbury" title="Cadbury">Cadbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/J._S._Fry_%26_Sons" title="J. S. Fry &amp; Sons">Fry's</a> faced controversy over the labor conditions in the Portuguese cacao industry in Africa. A 1908 report by a Cadbury agent described conditions as "de facto slavery."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWalker2008553&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker2008553-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While conditions somewhat improved with a boycott by chocolate makers,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Quaker&#95;Capitalists&#95;37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoeCoe2013Quaker_Capitalists-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELeissle201841&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeissle201841-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> slave labor among African cacao growers again gained public attention in the early 21st century.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELeissle201828&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeissle201828-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the 20th century, chocolate production further developed, with development of the <a href="/wiki/Tempered_chocolate" title="Tempered chocolate">tempering technique</a> to improve the snap and gloss of chocolate and the addition of <a href="/wiki/Lecithin" title="Lecithin">lecithin</a> to improve texture and consistency.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESnyderOlsenBrindle2008620&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESnyderOlsenBrindle2008620-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMossBadenoch200963&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMossBadenoch200963-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/White_chocolate" title="White chocolate">White</a> and <a href="/wiki/Couverture_chocolate" title="Couverture chocolate">couverture chocolate</a> were developed in the 20th century and the <a href="/wiki/Bean-to-bar" title="Bean-to-bar">bean-to-bar</a> trade model began.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett201910&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett201910-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGarronePietersSwinnen201691&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGarronePietersSwinnen201691-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELeissle201839&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeissle201839-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Types">Types</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Types_of_chocolate" title="Types of chocolate">Types of chocolate</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:L%C3%A4derach.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/L%C3%A4derach.jpg/250px-L%C3%A4derach.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/L%C3%A4derach.jpg/500px-L%C3%A4derach.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2784" data-file-height="1848" /></a><figcaption>Barks made of different varieties of chocolate</figcaption></figure>
<p>Several types of chocolate can be distinguished. Pure, unsweetened chocolate, often called "baking chocolate", contains primarily <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_solids" title="Cocoa solids">cocoa solids</a> and cocoa butter in varying proportions. Much of the chocolate consumed today is in the form of sweet chocolate, which combines chocolate with <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Eating_chocolate">Eating chocolate</h3></div>
<p>The traditional types of chocolate are <a href="/wiki/Dark_chocolate" title="Dark chocolate">dark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milk_chocolate" title="Milk chocolate">milk</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_chocolate" title="White chocolate">white</a>. All of them contain <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_butter" title="Cocoa butter">cocoa butter</a>, which is the ingredient defining the physical properties of chocolate (consistency and melting temperature). Plain (or dark) chocolate, as its name suggests, is a form of chocolate that is similar to pure <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_liquor" class="mw-redirect" title="Cocoa liquor">cocoa liquor</a>, although is usually made with a slightly higher proportion of cocoa butter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is simply defined by its cocoa percentage. In milk chocolate, the non-fat cocoa solids are partly or mostly replaced by <a href="/wiki/Milk_solid" class="mw-redirect" title="Milk solid">milk solids</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In white chocolate, they are all replaced by milk solids, hence its ivory color.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other forms of eating chocolate exist, these include <a href="/wiki/Raw_chocolate" title="Raw chocolate">raw chocolate</a> (made with unroasted beans) and <a href="/wiki/Ruby_chocolate" title="Ruby chocolate">ruby chocolate</a>. An additional popular form of eating chocolate, <a href="/wiki/Gianduja_(chocolate)" title="Gianduja (chocolate)">gianduja</a>, is made by incorporating <a href="/wiki/Hazelnut_butter" title="Hazelnut butter">nut paste</a> (typically hazelnut) to the chocolate paste.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other_types">Other types</h3></div>
<p>Other types of chocolate are used in baking and confectionery. These include <a href="/wiki/Baking_chocolate" title="Baking chocolate">baking chocolate</a> (often unsweetened), <a href="/wiki/Couverture_chocolate" title="Couverture chocolate">couverture chocolate</a> (used for coating), <a href="/wiki/Compound_chocolate" title="Compound chocolate">compound chocolate</a> (a lower-cost alternative) and <a href="/wiki/Modeling_chocolate" class="mw-redirect" title="Modeling chocolate">modeling chocolate</a>. Modeling chocolate is a chocolate paste made by melting chocolate and combining it with <a href="/wiki/Corn_syrup" title="Corn syrup">corn syrup</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glucose_syrup" title="Glucose syrup">glucose syrup</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Golden_syrup" title="Golden syrup">golden syrup</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Cacao">Cacao</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cocoa_Pods.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Cocoa_Pods.JPG/250px-Cocoa_Pods.JPG" decoding="async" width="190" height="285" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Cocoa_Pods.JPG/330px-Cocoa_Pods.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Cocoa_Pods.JPG/500px-Cocoa_Pods.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1536" /></a><figcaption>Chocolate is created from the cocoa bean. A cacao tree with fruit pods in various stages of ripening.</figcaption></figure><p>Chocolate is made from cocoa beans, the dried and often fermented seeds of the cacao tree (<i><a href="/wiki/Theobroma_cacao" title="Theobroma cacao">Theobroma cacao</a></i>), a small, 4–8&#160;m (13–26&#160;ft) tall <a href="/wiki/Evergreen" title="Evergreen">evergreen</a> tree native to South America. The most common <a href="/wiki/Genotype" title="Genotype">genotype</a> originated in the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_basin" title="Amazon basin">Amazon basin</a>, and was gradually transported by humans throughout South and Central America. Early forms of another genotype have also been found in what is now Venezuela. 
</p><p>The scientific name, <i><a href="/wiki/Theobroma" title="Theobroma">Theobroma</a></i>, means "food of the gods".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The fruit, called a cocoa pod, is ovoid, 15–30&#160;cm (6–12&#160;in) long and 8–10&#160;cm (3–4&#160;in) wide, ripening yellow to orange, and weighing about 500&#160;g (1.1&#160;lb) when ripe. 
</p><p>Cacao trees are small, understory trees that need rich, well-drained soils. They naturally grow within 20° of either side of the equator because they need about 2000&#160;mm of rainfall a year, and temperatures in the range of 21–32&#160;°C (70–90&#160;°F). Cacao trees cannot tolerate a temperature lower than 15&#160;°C (59&#160;°F).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The genome of the cacao tree was sequenced in 2010.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, cacao was understood to be divided into three varieties: Criollo, Forastero, and Trinitario. New genetic research has not found a genetic backing for this division, and it has identified eleven genetic clusters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Processing">Processing <span class="anchor" id="Harvesting"></span><span class="anchor" id="Refining"></span><span class="anchor" id="Processing"></span><span class="anchor" id="Chocolate&#95;liquor"></span></h2></div>
<p>Cocoa pods are harvested by cutting them from the tree using a <a href="/wiki/Machete" title="Machete">machete</a>, or by knocking them off the tree using a stick. Pods are harvested when they are ripe, as beans in unripe pods have a low cocoa butter content, or low sugar content, impacting the ultimate flavor.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cocoa_beans">Cocoa beans</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cocoa_farmer_David_Kebu_Jnr_holding_fermenting_cocoa_beans._(10687048615).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Cocoa_farmer_David_Kebu_Jnr_holding_fermenting_cocoa_beans._%2810687048615%29.jpg/250px-Cocoa_farmer_David_Kebu_Jnr_holding_fermenting_cocoa_beans._%2810687048615%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Cocoa_farmer_David_Kebu_Jnr_holding_fermenting_cocoa_beans._%2810687048615%29.jpg/500px-Cocoa_farmer_David_Kebu_Jnr_holding_fermenting_cocoa_beans._%2810687048615%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a><figcaption>Fermenting cocoa beans</figcaption></figure>
<p>The beans, which are <a href="/wiki/Asepsis" title="Asepsis">sterile</a> within their pods, and their surrounding pulp are removed from the pods and placed in piles or bins <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean_fermentation" title="Cocoa bean fermentation">to ferment</a>. <a href="/wiki/Micro-organisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Micro-organisms">Micro-organisms</a>, present naturally in the environment, ferment the seeds. <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">Yeasts</a> produce <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid_bacteria" title="Lactic acid bacteria">lactic acid bacteria</a> produce <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid_bacteria" title="Acetic acid bacteria">acetic acid bacteria</a> produce <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>. The fermentation process, which takes up to seven days, produces several flavor precursors, that eventually provide the chocolate taste.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>After fermentation, the beans are dried to prevent mold growth. Where the weather permits it, this is done by spreading the beans out in the sun for five to seven days.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-beans&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beans-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The dried beans are then transported to a chocolate manufacturing facility. The beans are cleaned (removing twigs, stones, and other debris), <a href="/wiki/Dry_roasting" title="Dry roasting">roasted</a>, and graded. Next, the shell of each bean is removed to extract the nib.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="From_nibs_to_chocolate">From nibs to chocolate</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ghirardelli_Chocolate_Square_9_2013-03-28.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ghirardelli_Chocolate_Square_9_2013-03-28.jpg/250px-Ghirardelli_Chocolate_Square_9_2013-03-28.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ghirardelli_Chocolate_Square_9_2013-03-28.jpg/500px-Ghirardelli_Chocolate_Square_9_2013-03-28.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a><figcaption>A chocolate mill (right) grinds and heats cocoa kernels into chocolate liquor. A <a href="/wiki/Melanger" title="Melanger">melanger</a> (left) mixes <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>, and other ingredients into the liquor.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Next, the nibs are ground, producing <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_liquor" title="Chocolate liquor">chocolate liquor</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The liquor can be further processed into cocoa solids and cocoa butter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tree&#95;61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tree-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Conche.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Conche.jpg/250px-Conche.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Conche.jpg/500px-Conche.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a><figcaption>A longitudinal conche</figcaption></figure>
<p>The penultimate process is called <a href="/wiki/Conching" title="Conching">conching</a>. A conche is a container filled with metal beads, which act as grinders. The refined and blended chocolate mass is kept in a liquid state by frictional heat. Before conching, chocolate has an uneven and gritty texture. The conching process produces cocoa and sugar particles smaller than the tongue can detect (typically around 20 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">μm</a>) and reduces rough edges, hence the smooth feel in the mouth. The length of the conching process determines the final smoothness and quality of the chocolate. After the process is complete, the chocolate mass is stored in tanks heated to about 45–50&#160;°C (113–122&#160;°F) until final processing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-conch&#95;62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-conch-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>After conching, chocolate is <a href="/wiki/Tempered_chocolate" title="Tempered chocolate">tempered</a> to crystallize a small amount of cocoa butter. Cocoa butter is a polymorphic fat with six different crystal forms, but only one of them—Form V—gives chocolate its characteristic snap, gloss, and stable texture. Tempering eliminates the undesirable crystal forms and promotes the formation of Form V.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett201995&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett201995-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett201981&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett201981-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>After chocolate has been tempered, it is molded into different shapes, including <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_bars" class="mw-redirect" title="Chocolate bars">chocolate bars</a> and chocolate chips.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Storage">Storage</h3></div>
<p>Chocolate is very sensitive to temperature and humidity. Ideal storage temperatures are between 15 and 17&#160;°C (59 and 63&#160;°F), with a relative humidity of less than 50%. If refrigerated or frozen without containment, chocolate can absorb enough moisture to cause a whitish discoloration, the result of fat or sugar crystals rising to the surface. Various types of "blooming" effects can occur if chocolate is stored or served improperly.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chocolate_bloom" title="Chocolate bloom">Chocolate bloom</a> is caused by storage temperature fluctuating or exceeding 24&#160;°C (75&#160;°F), while sugar bloom is caused by temperature below 15&#160;°C (59&#160;°F) or excess humidity. A fat bloom can be distinguished by touch; it disappears if the surface of affected chocolate is lightly rubbed. Although visually unappealing, chocolate suffering from bloom is safe for consumption and taste is unaffected.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bloom can be reversed by retempering the chocolate or using it for any use that requires melting the chocolate.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Chocolate is generally stored away from other foods, as it can absorb aromas. To avoid this, chocolate is packed or wrapped, then stored in darkness, in ideal humidity and temperature conditions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Health_effects">Health effects</h2></div>
