<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Nutrition">Nutrition</h3></div>
<p>One hundred grams of milk chocolate supplies 540 <a href="/wiki/Calories" class="mw-redirect" title="Calories">calories</a>. It is 59% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a> (52% as <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> and 3% as <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>), 30% fat and 8% <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">protein</a> (table). Approximately 65% of the fat in milk chocolate is <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated</a>, mainly <a href="/wiki/Palmitic_acid" title="Palmitic acid">palmitic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stearic_acid" title="Stearic acid">stearic acid</a>, while the predominant <a href="/wiki/Unsaturated_fat" title="Unsaturated fat">unsaturated fat</a> is <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic acid</a> (table).
</p><p>One hundred grams of milk chocolate is an <i>excellent source</i> (over 19% of the <a href="/wiki/Daily_Value" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a>, DV) of <a href="/wiki/Riboflavin" title="Riboflavin">riboflavin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12" title="Vitamin B12">vitamin B12</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dietary_minerals" class="mw-redirect" title="Dietary minerals">dietary minerals</a> <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>. Chocolate is a <i>good source</i> (10–19% DV) of <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Phytochemicals">Phytochemicals</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol#Research" title="Polyphenol">Polyphenol §&#160;Research</a></div>
<p>Chocolate contains <a href="/wiki/Polyphenol" title="Polyphenol">polyphenols</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Flavan-3-ol" title="Flavan-3-ol">flavan-3-ols</a> (catechins) and smaller amounts of other <a href="/wiki/Flavonoid" title="Flavonoid">flavonoids</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid30620683&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30620683-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It also contains <a href="/wiki/Alkaloid" title="Alkaloid">alkaloids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Theobromine" title="Theobromine">theobromine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phenethylamine" title="Phenethylamine">phenethylamine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which are under study for their potential effects in the body.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Heavy_metals">Heavy metals</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Chocolate02.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Chocolate02.jpg/250px-Chocolate02.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="240" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Chocolate02.jpg/500px-Chocolate02.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="768" /></a><figcaption>A chocolate bar in a bowl of melted chocolate</figcaption></figure>
<p>It is unlikely that chocolate consumption in small amounts causes <a href="/wiki/Lead_poisoning" title="Lead poisoning">lead poisoning</a>. Some studies have shown that lead may bind to cocoa shells, and contamination may occur during the manufacturing process.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rankin&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankin-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One study showed the mean lead level in milk chocolate candy bars was 0.027&#160;μg lead per gram of candy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rankin&#95;79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankin-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another study found that some chocolate purchased at U.S. <a href="/wiki/Supermarket" title="Supermarket">supermarkets</a> contained up to 0.965&#160;μg per gram, close to the international (voluntary) standard limit for lead in cocoa powder or beans, which is 1&#160;μg of lead per gram.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>In 2006, the U.S. FDA lowered by one-fifth the amount of lead permissible in candy, but compliance is only voluntary.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Studies concluded that "children, who are big consumers of chocolates, may be at risk of exceeding the daily limit of lead, [as] one 10 g cube of dark chocolate may contain as much as 20% of the daily lead oral limit. Moreover chocolate may not be the only source of lead in their nutrition"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and "chocolate might be a significant source of <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> and lead ingestion, particularly for children."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>According to a 2005 study, the average <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> concentration of cocoa beans is ≤ 0.5&#160;ng/g, which is one of the lowest reported values for a natural food.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rankin&#95;79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankin-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, during cultivation and production, chocolate may absorb lead from the environment (such as in atmospheric emissions of now unused leaded gasoline).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rankin&#95;79-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankin-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> recommended a tolerable weekly intake for cadmium of 2.5 <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">micrograms</a> per kg of body weight for Europeans, indicating that consuming chocolate products caused exposure of about 4% among all foods eaten.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-foodsafetynews/2021/lowers-cadmium&#95;85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foodsafetynews/2021/lowers-cadmium-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Maximum levels for baby foods and chocolate/cocoa products were established under Commission Regulation (EU) No 488/2014.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/1986_California_Proposition_65" title="1986 California Proposition 65">1986 California Proposition 65</a> requires a warning label on chocolate products having more than 4.1&#160;mg of cadmium per daily serving of a single product.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-confectionerynews/cadmium-lead-chocolate&#95;88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-confectionerynews/cadmium-lead-chocolate-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-consumerreports/a8480295550&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-consumerreports/a8480295550-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-19393210.2017.1420700&#95;90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19393210.2017.1420700-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Caffeine">Caffeine</h3></div>
<p>One tablespoonful (5 grams) of dry unsweetened <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_powder" class="mw-redirect" title="Cocoa powder">cocoa powder</a> has 12.1&#160;mg of caffeine<sup id="cite&#95;ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and a 25-g single serving of dark chocolate has 22.4&#160;mg of caffeine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is much less than the amount found in coffee, of which a single 7&#160;oz. (200&#160;ml) serving may contain 80–175&#160;mg of caffeine,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bunker&#95;93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bunker-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> though studies have shown psychoactive effects in caffeine doses as low as 9&#160;mg, and a dose as low as 12.5&#160;mg was shown to have effects on cognitive performance.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Theobromine_and_oxalate">Theobromine and oxalate</h3></div>
<p>Chocolate may be a factor for <a href="/wiki/Heartburn" title="Heartburn">heartburn</a> in some people because one of its constituents, theobromine, may affect the <a href="/wiki/Lower_esophageal_sphincter" class="mw-redirect" title="Lower esophageal sphincter">esophageal sphincter</a> muscle in a way that permits stomach acids to enter the <a href="/wiki/Esophagus" title="Esophagus">esophagus</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Latif&#95;95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Latif-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Theobromine_poisoning" title="Theobromine poisoning">Theobromine poisoning</a> is an overdosage reaction to the bitter alkaloid, which happens more frequently in <a href="/wiki/Domestic_animals" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic animals">domestic animals</a> than humans. However, daily intake of 50–100 g cocoa (0.8–1.5 g theobromine) by humans has been associated with sweating, trembling, and severe headache.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Chocolate and cocoa contain moderate to high amounts of <a href="/wiki/Oxalate" title="Oxalate">oxalate</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which may increase the risk of <a href="/wiki/Kidney_stone" class="mw-redirect" title="Kidney stone">kidney stones</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Non-human_animals">Non-human animals</h4></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Theobromine_poisoning" title="Theobromine poisoning">Theobromine poisoning</a></div>
<p>In sufficient amounts, the theobromine found in chocolate is <a href="/wiki/Toxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Toxic">toxic</a> to animals such as cats, dogs, horses, parrots, and small rodents because they are unable to <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolize</a> the chemical effectively.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If animals are fed chocolate, the theobromine may remain in the circulation for up to 20&#160;hours, possibly causing <a href="/wiki/Epileptic_seizure" class="mw-redirect" title="Epileptic seizure">epileptic seizures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attacks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Internal_bleeding" title="Internal bleeding">internal bleeding</a>, and eventually death. Medical treatment performed by a <a href="/wiki/Veterinarian" title="Veterinarian">veterinarian</a> involves inducing <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a> within two hours of ingestion and administration of <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine" title="Benzodiazepine">benzodiazepines</a> or <a href="/wiki/Barbiturate" title="Barbiturate">barbiturates</a> for seizures, <a href="/wiki/Antiarrhythmic_agents" class="mw-redirect" title="Antiarrhythmic agents">antiarrhythmics</a> for <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrhythmia" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiac arrhythmia">heart arrhythmias</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Forced_diuresis" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced diuresis">fluid diuresis</a>.
</p><p>A typical 20-kilogram (44&#160;lb) dog will normally experience great intestinal distress after eating less than 240 grams (8.5&#160;oz) of dark chocolate, but will not necessarily experience <a href="/wiki/Bradycardia" title="Bradycardia">bradycardia</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tachycardia" title="Tachycardia">tachycardia</a> unless it eats at least a half a kilogram (1.1&#160;lb) of milk chocolate. Dark chocolate has 2 to 5 times more theobromine and thus is more dangerous to dogs. According to the <a href="/wiki/Merck_Veterinary_Manual" title="Merck Veterinary Manual">Merck Veterinary Manual</a>, approximately 1.3&#160;grams of baker's chocolate per kilogram of a dog's body weight (0.02&#160;oz/lb) is sufficient to cause symptoms of toxicity. For example, a typical 25-gram (0.88&#160;oz) baker's chocolate bar would be enough to bring about symptoms in a 20-kilogram (44&#160;lb) dog.  In the 20th century, there were reports that <a href="/wiki/Mulch" title="Mulch">mulch</a> made from cocoa bean shells is dangerous to dogs and livestock.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Research">Research</h3></div>
<p>Commonly consumed chocolate is high in <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>, which are associated with an increased risk for <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> when chocolate is consumed in excess.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Overall evidence is insufficient to determine the relationship between chocolate consumption and <a href="/wiki/Acne" title="Acne">acne</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bhate2013&#95;104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bhate2013-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Diet2010&#95;105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diet2010-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Various studies point not to chocolate, but to the high <a href="/wiki/Glycemic" title="Glycemic">glycemic</a> nature of certain foods, like sugar, corn syrup, and other simple carbohydrates, as potential causes of acne,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bhate2013&#95;104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bhate2013-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Diet2010&#95;105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diet2010-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Acta2013&#95;106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Acta2013-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mahmood2014&#95;107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahmood2014-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> along with other possible dietary factors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-bhate2013&#95;104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bhate2013-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Food, including chocolate, is not typically viewed as <a href="/wiki/Addiction" title="Addiction">addictive</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rogers2000&#95;109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers2000-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some people, however, may want or crave chocolate,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rogers2000&#95;109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers2000-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> leading to a self-described term, <i>chocoholic</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rogers2000&#95;109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers2000-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>By some <a href="/wiki/Urban_legend" title="Urban legend">popular myths</a>, chocolate is considered to be a <a href="/wiki/Mood_(psychology)" title="Mood (psychology)">mood enhancer</a>, such as by increasing <a href="/wiki/Aphrodisiac" title="Aphrodisiac">sex drive</a> or stimulating <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a>, but there is little scientific evidence that such effects are consistent among all chocolate consumers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Scholey&#95;112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scholey-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If mood improvement from eating chocolate occurs, there is not enough research to indicate whether it results from the favorable flavor or from the <a href="/wiki/Stimulant" title="Stimulant">stimulant</a> effects of its constituents, such as caffeine, theobromine, or their parent molecule, <a href="/wiki/Xanthine" title="Xanthine">methylxanthine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Scholey&#95;112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scholey-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2019 review reported that chocolate consumption does not improve <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depressive mood</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Veronese2019&#95;113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veronese2019-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Reviews support a short-term effect of lowering blood pressure by consuming cocoa products, but there is limited evidence of long-term cardiovascular health benefit.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-milliron&#95;114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-milliron-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chocolate and cocoa are under preliminary research to determine if consumption affects the risk of certain <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiovascular diseases">cardiovascular diseases</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Daily consumption of cocoa flavanols (minimum dose of 200&#160;mg) appears to benefit <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">platelet</a> and vascular function.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-arranz&#95;117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arranz-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-sudano&#95;118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sudano-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Labeling">Labeling</h2></div>
<p>Some manufacturers provide the percentage of chocolate in a finished chocolate confection as a label quoting percentage of "cocoa" or "cacao". This refers to the combined percentage of both cocoa solids and cocoa butter in the bar, not just the percentage of cocoa solids.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Belgian <a href="/wiki/AMBAO" title="AMBAO">AMBAO</a> certification mark indicates that no non-cocoa vegetable fats have been used in making the chocolate.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nyt&#95;121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-just-food&#95;122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-just-food-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A long-standing dispute between Britain on the one hand and Belgium and France over British use of vegetable fats in chocolate ended in 2000 with the adoption of new standards which permitted the use of up to five percent vegetable fats in clearly labelled products.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BBC&#95;news&#95;123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_news-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Chocolates that are <a href="/wiki/Organic_food" title="Organic food">organic</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Fairtrade_certification" class="mw-redirect" title="Fairtrade certification">fair trade certified</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> carry labels accordingly.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Legal_definitions">Legal definitions</h3></div>
<p>In the US, the Food and Drug Administration does not allow a product to be referred to as "chocolate" if the product contains any of these ingredients.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the EU a product can be sold as chocolate if it contains up to 5% vegetable oil, and must be labeled as "family milk chocolate" rather than "milk chocolate" if it contains 20% milk.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to Canadian Food and Drug Regulations, a "chocolate product" is a food product that is sourced from at least one "cocoa product" and contains at least one of the following: "chocolate, bittersweet chocolate, semi-sweet chocolate, dark chocolate, sweet chocolate, milk chocolate, or white chocolate". A "cocoa product" is defined as a food product that is sourced from cocoa beans and contains "cocoa nibs, cocoa liquor, cocoa mass, unsweetened chocolate, bitter chocolate, chocolate liquor, cocoa, low-fat cocoa, cocoa powder, or low-fat cocoa powder".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Industry">Industry</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_industry" title="Chocolate industry">Chocolate industry</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ghirardelli_Chocolate_Shop_Inside,_SF,_CA,_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Ghirardelli_Chocolate_Shop_Inside%2C_SF%2C_CA%2C_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg/250px-Ghirardelli_Chocolate_Shop_Inside%2C_SF%2C_CA%2C_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="203" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Ghirardelli_Chocolate_Shop_Inside%2C_SF%2C_CA%2C_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg/500px-Ghirardelli_Chocolate_Shop_Inside%2C_SF%2C_CA%2C_jjron_25.03.2012.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1346" data-file-height="1094" /></a><figcaption>Packaged chocolate in the <a href="/wiki/Ghirardelli_Chocolate_Company" title="Ghirardelli Chocolate Company">Ghirardelli Chocolate Company</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Chocolate is a steadily growing, <a href="/wiki/US$" class="mw-redirect" title="US$">US$</a>50&#160;billion-a-year worldwide business as of 2009.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of 2006, Europe accounted for 45% of the world's chocolate revenue,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the US spent $20&#160;billion in 2013.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Big_Chocolate" title="Big Chocolate">Big Chocolate</a> is a grouping of major international chocolate companies in Europe and the US. In 2004, Mars and Hershey's alone accounted for two-thirds of US production.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Early in the 21st century, roughly two-thirds of the world's cocoa was produced in West Africa, with 43% sourced from Ivory Coast, which commonly used child labor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BBC&#95;134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> That year some 50&#160;million people around the world depended on cocoa as a source of livelihood.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pantograph&#95;135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pantograph-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of 2007<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Chocolate&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> in the UK, most chocolatiers purchase their chocolate from them, to melt, mold and package to their own design.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BBCR4&#95;136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCR4-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The two main jobs associated with creating chocolate candy are chocolate makers and chocolatiers. Chocolate makers use harvested cocoa beans and other ingredients to produce <a href="/wiki/Couverture_chocolate" title="Couverture chocolate">couverture chocolate</a> (covering). Chocolatiers use the finished couverture to make chocolate candies (bars and <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_truffle" title="Chocolate truffle">truffles</a>).
</p><p>Production costs can be decreased by reducing cocoa solids content or by substituting cocoa butter with another fat. Cocoa growers object to allowing the resulting food to be called "chocolate", due to the risk of lower demand for their crops.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pantograph&#95;135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pantograph-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Manufacturers">Manufacturers</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_bean-to-bar_chocolate_manufacturers" title="List of bean-to-bar chocolate manufacturers">List of bean-to-bar chocolate manufacturers</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:FD_3.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/FD_3.jpg/250px-FD_3.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="270" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/FD_3.jpg/500px-FD_3.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="709" data-file-height="765" /></a><figcaption>Chocolate with various fillings</figcaption></figure>
<p>Chocolate manufacturers produce a range of products from chocolate bars to <a href="/wiki/Fudge" title="Fudge">fudge</a>. Large manufacturers of chocolate products include <a href="/wiki/Cadbury" title="Cadbury">Cadbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferrero_SpA" title="Ferrero SpA">Ferrero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guylian" title="Guylian">Guylian</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Hershey_Company" title="The Hershey Company">The Hershey Company</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lindt_%26_Spr%C3%BCngli" class="mw-redirect" title="Lindt &amp; Sprüngli">Lindt &amp; Sprüngli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars,_Incorporated" class="mw-redirect" title="Mars, Incorporated">Mars, Incorporated</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milka" title="Milka">Milka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuhaus_(chocolatier)" title="Neuhaus (chocolatier)">Neuhaus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Suchard" title="Philippe Suchard">Suchard</a>.
</p><p>Guylian is best known for its chocolate sea shells; Cadbury for its <a href="/wiki/Cadbury_Dairy_Milk" title="Cadbury Dairy Milk">Dairy Milk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cadbury_Creme_Egg" title="Cadbury Creme Egg">Creme Egg</a>. The Hershey Company, the largest chocolate manufacturer in North America, produces the <a href="/wiki/Hershey_Bar" class="mw-redirect" title="Hershey Bar">Hershey Bar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hershey%27s_Kisses" title="Hershey&#39;s Kisses">Hershey's Kisses</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-paul&#95;137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paul-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mars Incorporated, a large privately owned U.S. corporation, produces <a href="/wiki/Mars_Bar" class="mw-redirect" title="Mars Bar">Mars Bar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way_bar" class="mw-redirect" title="Milky Way bar">Milky Way</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%26M%27s" title="M&amp;M&#39;s">M&amp;M's</a>, <a href="/wiki/Twix" title="Twix">Twix</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Snickers" title="Snickers">Snickers</a>. <a href="/wiki/Lindt_%26_Spr%C3%BCngli" class="mw-redirect" title="Lindt &amp; Sprüngli">Lindt</a> is known for its <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_truffles" class="mw-redirect" title="Chocolate truffles">truffle balls</a> and gold foil-wrapped Easter bunnies.
</p><p>Food conglomerates <a href="/wiki/Nestl%C3%A9" title="Nestlé">Nestlé</a> SA and <a href="/wiki/Mondelez_International" title="Mondelez International">Mondelēz</a> both have chocolate brands. Nestlé acquired <a href="/wiki/Rowntree%27s" title="Rowntree&#39;s">Rowntree's</a> in 1988 and now markets chocolates under their brand, including <a href="/wiki/Smarties" title="Smarties">Smarties</a> (a chocolate candy) and <a href="/wiki/Kit_Kat" title="Kit Kat">Kit Kat</a> (a chocolate bar); Kraft Foods through its 1990 acquisition of Jacobs Suchard, now owns Milka and Suchard.  Fry's, Trebor Basset and the <a href="/wiki/Fair_trade" title="Fair trade">fair trade</a> brand <a href="/wiki/Green_%26_Black%27s" title="Green &amp; Black&#39;s">Green &amp; Black's</a> also belongs to the group.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Child_labor_in_cocoa_harvesting">Child labor in cocoa harvesting</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Child_labour_in_cocoa_production" title="Child labour in cocoa production">Child labour in cocoa production</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_production_in_Ivory_Coast" title="Cocoa production in Ivory Coast">Cocoa production in Ivory Coast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_production_in_Ghana" title="Cocoa production in Ghana">Cocoa production in Ghana</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Chuao_003.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Chuao_003.JPG/250px-Chuao_003.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Chuao_003.JPG/448px-Chuao_003.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/32/Chuao_003.JPG 2x" data-file-width="448" data-file-height="336" /></a><figcaption>Child drying cacao in <a href="/wiki/Chuao" title="Chuao">Chuao</a>, Venezuela</figcaption></figure>
<p>The widespread use of children in cocoa production is controversial, not only for the concerns about <a href="/wiki/Child_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Child labor">child labor</a> and exploitation, but also because according to a 2002 estimate, up to 12,000 of the 200,000 children then working in the <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a> cocoa industry<sup id="cite&#95;ref-accuses&#95;companies&#95;138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-accuses_companies-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> may have been victims of <a href="/wiki/Trafficking_of_children" title="Trafficking of children">trafficking</a> or <a href="/wiki/Child_slavery" title="Child slavery">slavery</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-fortune2018&#95;139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fortune2018-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most attention on this subject has focused on <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, which collectively supplies 69 percent of the world's cocoa,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-WCF&#95;2010&#95;update&#95;140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCF_2010_update-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the Ivory Coast in particular, which supplies 35 percent of the world's cocoa.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-WCF&#95;2010&#95;update&#95;140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCF_2010_update-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Thirty percent of children under age 15 in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> are child laborers, mostly in agricultural activities including cocoa farming.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rooting&#95;141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rooting-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Major chocolate producers, such as <a href="/wiki/Nestl%C3%A9" title="Nestlé">Nestlé</a>, buy cocoa at <a href="/wiki/Commodities_exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="Commodities exchange">commodities exchanges</a> where Ivorian cocoa is mixed with other cocoa.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-oxfam&#95;142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxfam-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2017, approximately 2.1&#160;million children in Ghana and Ivory Coast were involved in farming cocoa, carrying heavy loads, clearing forests, and being exposed to pesticides.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-reuters2017&#95;143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters2017-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of 2018, a 3-year <a href="/wiki/Pilot_experiment" title="Pilot experiment">pilot program</a> – conducted by Nestlé with 26,000 farmers mostly located in Ivory Coast – observed a 51% decrease in the number of children doing hazardous jobs in cocoa farming.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-balch&#95;144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-balch-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Labor" title="United States Department of Labor">US Department of Labor</a> formed the Child Labor Cocoa Coordinating Group as a public-private partnership with the governments of Ghana and Ivory Coast to address child labor practices in the cocoa industry.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The International Cocoa Initiative involving major cocoa manufacturers established the Child Labor Monitoring and Remediation System to monitor thousands of farms in Ghana and Ivory Coast for child labor conditions,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-balch&#95;144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-balch-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-reuters2017&#95;143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters2017-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but the program reached less than 20% of the child laborers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2018, the Cocoa Barometer report stated: "Not a single company or government is anywhere near reaching the sector-wide objective of the elimination of child labor, and not even near their commitments of a 70% reduction of child labor by 2020". They cited persistent poverty, the absence of schools, increasing world cocoa demand, more <a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">intensive farming</a> of cocoa, and continued exploitation of child labor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-balch&#95;144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-balch-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-cb2018&#95;147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cb2018-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Fair_trade">Fair trade</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fair_trade_cocoa" title="Fair trade cocoa">Fair trade cocoa</a></div>
<p>In the 2000s, some chocolate producers began to engage in <a href="/wiki/Fair_trade" title="Fair trade">fair trade</a> initiatives, to address concerns about the low pay of cocoa laborers in developing countries. Traditionally, Africa and other developing countries received low prices for their exported commodities such as cocoa, which caused poverty. Fairtrade seeks to establish a system of direct trade from developing countries to counteract this system.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One solution for fair labor practices is for farmers to become part of an <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_cooperative" title="Agricultural cooperative">agricultural cooperative</a>. Cooperatives pay farmers a fair price for their cocoa so farmers have enough money for food, clothes, and school fees.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>One of the main tenets of fair trade is that farmers receive a fair price, but this does not mean that the larger amount of money paid for fair trade cocoa goes directly to the farmers. The effectiveness of fair trade has been questioned. In a 2014 article, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> stated that workers on fair trade farms have a lower standard of living than on similar farms outside the fair trade system<sup id="cite&#95;ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> based on a study of tea and coffee farmers in Uganda and Ethiopia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Usage_and_consumption">Usage and consumption</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cadbury-Dairy-Milk-Caramel-Bar.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Cadbury-Dairy-Milk-Caramel-Bar.jpg/330px-Cadbury-Dairy-Milk-Caramel-Bar.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="155" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Cadbury-Dairy-Milk-Caramel-Bar.jpg/500px-Cadbury-Dairy-Milk-Caramel-Bar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Cadbury-Dairy-Milk-Caramel-Bar.jpg/960px-Cadbury-Dairy-Milk-Caramel-Bar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3180" data-file-height="1760" /></a><figcaption>A Cadbury chocolate bar</figcaption></figure><p>Chocolate is sold in <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_bar" title="Chocolate bar">chocolate bars</a>, which come in <a href="/wiki/Dark_chocolate" title="Dark chocolate">dark chocolate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milk_chocolate" title="Milk chocolate">milk chocolate</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_chocolate" title="White chocolate">white chocolate</a> varieties. Some bars that are mostly chocolate have other ingredients blended into the chocolate, such as nuts, raisins, or crisped rice. Chocolate is used as an ingredient in a huge variety of bars, which typically contain various confectionary ingredients (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Nougat" title="Nougat">nougat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wafer" title="Wafer">wafers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caramel" title="Caramel">caramel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nut_(fruit)" title="Nut (fruit)">nuts</a>) which are coated in chocolate.
</p><figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Blackout_cake.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Blackout_cake.jpg/250px-Blackout_cake.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="190" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Blackout_cake.jpg/330px-Blackout_cake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Blackout_cake.jpg/500px-Blackout_cake.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2400" /></a><figcaption>Chocolate cake with chocolate frosting</figcaption></figure>
<p>Chocolate is used as a flavoring product in many <a href="/wiki/Dessert" title="Dessert">desserts</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_cake" title="Chocolate cake">chocolate cakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_brownie" title="Chocolate brownie">chocolate brownies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_mousse" class="mw-redirect" title="Chocolate mousse">chocolate mousse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_chip_cookie" title="Chocolate chip cookie">chocolate chip cookies</a>. Numerous types of <a href="/wiki/Candy" title="Candy">candy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Snack" title="Snack">snacks</a> contain chocolate, either as a filling (e.g., <a href="/wiki/M%26M%27s" title="M&amp;M&#39;s">M&amp;M's</a>) or as a coating (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Chocolate-coated_raisins" class="mw-redirect" title="Chocolate-coated raisins">chocolate-coated raisins</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chocolate-coated_peanuts" class="mw-redirect" title="Chocolate-coated peanuts">chocolate-coated peanuts</a>).
</p><p>Some non-alcoholic beverages contain chocolate, such as <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_milk" title="Chocolate milk">chocolate milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hot_chocolate" title="Hot chocolate">hot chocolate</a>, chocolate <a href="/wiki/Milkshakes" class="mw-redirect" title="Milkshakes">milkshakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tejate" title="Tejate">tejate</a>. Some alcoholic liqueurs are flavored with chocolate, such as <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_liqueur" title="Chocolate liqueur">chocolate liqueur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Creme_de_cacao" class="mw-redirect" title="Creme de cacao">crème de cacao</a>. Chocolate is a popular flavor of <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream" title="Ice cream">ice cream</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_pudding" title="Chocolate pudding">pudding</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chocolate_sauce" class="mw-redirect" title="Chocolate sauce">chocolate sauce</a> is a commonly added as a topping on ice cream <a href="/wiki/Sundae" title="Sundae">sundaes</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Caff%C3%A8_mocha" title="Caffè mocha">caffè mocha</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Espresso" title="Espresso">espresso</a> beverage containing chocolate.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Eating_experience">Eating experience</h3></div>
<p>The experience of eating chocolate varies with the ingredients used. More sugary chocolates have a flavor that is more immediately apparent, while chocolates with higher cocoa percentages have flavors that take longer to be perceived but stay on the palate for longer. These chocolates with more cocoa are increasingly bitter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESegnit2010&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailsflavourthesaurus0000segnpage12mode2up&#95;13&#93;&#95;152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegnit2010[httpsarchiveorgdetailsflavourthesaurus0000segnpage12mode2up_13]-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</h2></div><p>
Chocolate is perceived to be different things at different times, including a sweet treat, a luxury product, a consumer good and a mood enhancer,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHackenesch201718&#95;153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackenesch201718-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the latter reputation in part driven by marketing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMartinSampeck2021&#95;154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartinSampeck2021-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chocolate is a popular metaphor for the black racial category.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHackenesch201710&#95;155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackenesch201710-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has connotations of transgression and sexuality<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHackenesch201715&#95;156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHackenesch201715-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEScarpellini2016125&#95;157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScarpellini2016125-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and is gendered as feminine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMathias2022530&#95;158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMathias2022530-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the US there is a cultural practice of women consuming chocolate in secret; alone and with other women.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMcCabede&#95;Waal&#95;Malefyt202028&#95;159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCabede_Waal_Malefyt202028-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Children use chocolate as a euphemism for feces.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMoore200553&#95;160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore200553-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chocolate is popularly understood to have "exotic" origins,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMartinSampeck202148–49&#95;161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartinSampeck202148–49-161"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In China, chocolate is considered "<a href="/wiki/Shanghuo" title="Shanghuo">heaty</a>", and avoided in hot weather.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEAllen201024&#95;162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen201024-162"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>161<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p><figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg/250px-Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="186" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg/330px-Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg/500px-Elmer_Valentine_boxed_chocolates.jpg 2x" data-file-width="751" data-file-height="736" /></a><figcaption>A gift box of chocolates, which is a common gift for <a href="/wiki/Valentine%27s_Day" title="Valentine&#39;s Day">Valentine's Day</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Chocolate is associated with festivals such as <a href="/wiki/Easter_customs" class="mw-redirect" title="Easter customs">Easter</a>, when molded chocolate rabbits and eggs are traditionally given in Christian communities, and <a href="/wiki/Hanukkah" title="Hanukkah">Hanukkah</a>, when chocolate coins are given in Jewish communities. Chocolate <a href="/wiki/Heart_(symbol)" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart (symbol)">hearts</a> and chocolate in heart-shaped boxes are popular on <a href="/wiki/Valentine%27s_Day" title="Valentine&#39;s Day">Valentine's Day</a> and are often presented along with flowers and a <a href="/wiki/Greeting_card" title="Greeting card">greeting card</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chocolates&#95;34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chocolates-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Boxes of filled chocolates quickly became associated with the holiday.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chocolates&#95;34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chocolates-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chocolate is an acceptable gift on other holidays and on occasions such as birthdays. Many confectioners make holiday-specific chocolate candies. Chocolate <a href="/wiki/Easter_eggs" class="mw-redirect" title="Easter eggs">Easter eggs</a> or rabbits and <a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus" title="Santa Claus">Santa Claus</a> figures are two examples. Such confections can be solid, hollow, or filled with sweets or fondant.
</p><p>In 1964, <a href="/wiki/Roald_Dahl" title="Roald Dahl">Roald Dahl</a> published a children's novel titled <i><a href="/wiki/Charlie_and_the_Chocolate_Factory" title="Charlie and the Chocolate Factory">Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</a></i>. The novel centers on a poor boy named Charlie Bucket who takes a tour through the greatest chocolate factory in the world, owned by the eccentric <a href="/wiki/Willy_Wonka" title="Willy Wonka">Willy Wonka</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>163<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Two film adaptations of the novel were produced: <i><a href="/wiki/Willy_Wonka_%26_the_Chocolate_Factory" title="Willy Wonka &amp; the Chocolate Factory">Willy Wonka &amp; the Chocolate Factory</a></i> (1971) and <i><a href="/wiki/Charlie_and_the_Chocolate_Factory_(film)" title="Charlie and the Chocolate Factory (film)">Charlie and the Chocolate Factory</a></i> (2005). A third adaptation, an origin prequel film titled <i><a href="/wiki/Wonka_(film)" title="Wonka (film)">Wonka</a></i>, was released in 2023.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>164<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Chocolat_(novel)" title="Chocolat (novel)">Chocolat</a></i>, a 1999 novel by <a href="/wiki/Joanne_Harris" title="Joanne Harris">Joanne Harris</a>, was adapted for film in <i><a href="/wiki/Chocolat_(2000_film)" title="Chocolat (2000 film)">Chocolat</a></i> which was released a year later.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some artists have utilized chocolate in their art; <a href="/wiki/Dieter_Roth" title="Dieter Roth">Dieter Roth</a> was influential in this beginning with his works in the 1960s casting human and animal figures in chocolate, which used the chocolate's inevitable decay to comment on contemporary attitudes towards the permanence of museum displays. Other works have played on the audience's ability to consume displayed chocolate, encouraged in <a href="/wiki/Sonja_Alh%C3%A4user" title="Sonja Alhäuser">Sonja Alhäuser</a>'s <i>Exhibition Basics</i> (2001) and painfully disallowed in <a href="/wiki/Edward_Ruscha" title="Edward Ruscha">Edward Ruscha</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Chocolate_Room" title="Chocolate Room">Chocolate Room</a></i> (1970). In the 1980s and 90s, performance artists <a href="/wiki/Karen_Finley" title="Karen Finley">Karen Finley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Janine_Antoni" title="Janine Antoni">Janine Antoni</a> used chocolate's cultural popular associations of excrement and consumption, and desirability respectively to comment on the status of women in society.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>166<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Flavors">Flavors</h2></div>
<p><i>Mint chocolate</i> (or <i>chocolate mint</i>) is an individual flavor of chocolate, made by adding a <a href="/wiki/Mentha" title="Mentha">mint</a> flavoring, such as <a href="/wiki/Peppermint" title="Peppermint">peppermint</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spearmint" title="Spearmint">spearmint</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Cr%C3%A8me_de_menthe" title="Crème de menthe">crème de menthe</a>, to chocolate. Mint chocolate can be found in a wide variety of confectionery items, such as <a href="/wiki/Candy" title="Candy">candy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mint_(candy)" title="Mint (candy)">mints</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cookie" title="Cookie">cookies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mint_chocolate_chip" title="Mint chocolate chip">mint chocolate chip</a> ice cream, <a href="/wiki/Hot_chocolate" title="Hot chocolate">hot chocolate</a>, and others.  It is also marketed in a non-edible format in <a href="/wiki/Cosmetics" title="Cosmetics">cosmetics</a> with a distinctive mint fragrance. The chocolate component can be <a href="/wiki/Milk_chocolate" title="Milk chocolate">milk chocolate</a>, regular <a href="/wiki/Dark_chocolate" title="Dark chocolate">dark chocolate</a>, or <a href="/wiki/White_chocolate" title="White chocolate">white chocolate</a>; due to this, mint chocolate has no one specific flavour, and so each chocolate-plus-flavor combination can be unique. The U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Confectioners_Association" title="National Confectioners Association">National Confectioners Association</a> lists February 19 as "Chocolate Mint Day".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>167<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
